<p>The <b>University of Mannheim</b> (in German: <i><b>Universität Mannheim</b></i>), also known as <b>UMA</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Public_university" title="Public university">public</a> <a href="/wiki/Research_university" title="Research university">research university</a> situated in <a href="/wiki/Mannheim" title="Mannheim">Mannheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baden-W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Baden-Württemberg">Baden-Württemberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>. Founded in 1967 the university has its origins in the 1763 established <i>Theodoro Palatinae</i> (Palatine Academy of the Sciences Mannheim), which was founded by the later Duke of <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Theodore,_Elector_of_Bavaria" title="Charles Theodore, Elector of Bavaria">Charles Theodor</a>, as well as the <i>Handelshochschule</i> (Commercial College Mannheim), which was initiated by Mannheim's senior mayor <a href="/wiki/Otto_Beck" title="Otto Beck">Otto Beck</a> and Heidelberg's professor for Economics Eberhard Gothein in 1907.</p>
<p>The University offers a wide range of undergraduate and graduate programs as well as Ph.D degrees within <a href="/wiki/Business_administration" title="Business administration">business administration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">law</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_sciences" title="Social sciences">social sciences</a>, <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Information_systems" title="Information systems">information systems</a> – all with an interdisciplinary and international focus. The University of Mannheim's campus is located in the city center of Mannheim and mainly centers on its main campus – the <a href="/wiki/Mannheim_Palace" title="Mannheim Palace">Mannheim Palace</a> (completed in 1760). The university has around 12,362 full-time students, 800 scholars enrolled, 840 academic staff and a total income of more than €120 million in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> It is organized into 5 academic departments and 2 academic colleges.</p>
<p>The UMA is considered to have by far the best and most prestigious business school in Germany and is consistently ranked #1 in national university rankings and among the top <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Business_schools" title="Business schools">business schools</a> worldwide for its business administration and economics programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wiwo.de_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-wiwo.de-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Moreover, the university's programs for social sciences, politics as well as business informatics rank nationwide within the Top 3 and its programs for law and computer science within the Top 10.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lto_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-lto-11">[11]</a></sup> The 2015 <a href="/wiki/QS_World_University_Rankings" title="QS World University Rankings">QS World University Rankings</a> ranked the UMA among the best one hundred universities within the disciplines of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_Sciences" title="Social Sciences">Social Sciences &amp; Management</a>, <a href="/wiki/Accounting" title="Accounting">Accounting</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Finance" title="Finance">Finance</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Business_Administration" title="Business Administration">Business Administration</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Management" title="Management">Management</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">Economics</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Econometrics" title="Econometrics">Econometrics</a>, as well as among the Top 50 universities within the discipline of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_Sciences" title="Political Sciences">Political Sciences</a>. Furthermore, the University of Mannheim is placed 83rd with regard to global employer reputation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Times_Higher_Education_World_University_Rankings" title="Times Higher Education World University Rankings">THE</a> world university ranking in 2016 ranked the University of Mannheim 102nd worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>The University of Mannheim is a member of the <a href="/wiki/German_Universities_Excellence_Initiative" title="German Universities Excellence Initiative">German Universities Excellence Initiative</a>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Association_of_Universities" title="International Association of Universities">International Association of Universities</a>, the <a href="/wiki/European_Network_for_Training_Economic_Research" title="European Network for Training Economic Research">European Network for Training Economic Research</a>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Business_Education_Alliance" title="International Business Education Alliance">International Business Education Alliance</a> (IBEA), the <a href="/wiki/Council_on_Business_%26_Society" title="Council on Business &amp; Society">Council on Business &amp; Society</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Forschungsgemeinschaft" title="Deutsche Forschungsgemeinschaft">German Research Foundation (DFG)</a>, and it is accredited by the <a href="/wiki/European_Quality_Improvement_System" title="European Quality Improvement System">European Quality Improvement System</a> (EQUIS), the <a href="/wiki/Association_to_Advance_Collegiate_Schools_of_Business" title="Association to Advance Collegiate Schools of Business">Association to Advance Collegiate Schools of Business</a> (AACSB) as well as the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_MBAs" title="Association of MBAs">Association of MBAs</a> (AMBA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Mannheim&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_University_of_Mannheim" title="History of the University of Mannheim">History of the University of Mannheim</a>.</div>
<p>The University of Mannheim has no clearly defined foundation date. While the University as it is known today was officially founded in 1967, its roots can be dated back to the 18th century established <i>Theodoro Palatinae</i> (Palatine Academy of the Sciences Mannheim) and the Handelshochschule (Commercial College Mannheim) that dates back to the beginning of the 20th century. Mannheim's history is closely tied to the history of its main campus – the Mannheim Baroque Palace.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Mannheim&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mannheim_Palace" title="Mannheim Palace">Mannheim Palace</a> itself dates back to the early 18th century. The city of Mannheim, founded in 1606, was fortified and at the present site of the Mannheim Palace a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fortress" title="Fortress">fortress</a> called <i>Friedrichsburg</i> was located, sometimes serving as alternative residence for the Elector, one of the most important territorial princes of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>. When <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elector_Palatine" title="Elector Palatine">Elector Palatine</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_III_Philip,_Elector_Palatine" title="Charles III Philip, Elector Palatine">Karl Philip III.</a> had confessional controversies with the inhabitants of his capital city <a href="/wiki/Heidelberg" title="Heidelberg">Heidelberg</a>, he decided to appoint Mannheim <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electorate_of_the_Palatinate" title="Electorate of the Palatinate">the Palatinate</a>'s new capital in year 1720.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Schloss_Mannheim_1725_v_J_C_Froimon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1353" data-file-width="2117" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Schloss_Mannheim_1725_v_J_C_Froimon.jpg/240px-Schloss_Mannheim_1725_v_J_C_Froimon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Schloss_Mannheim_1725_v_J_C_Froimon.jpg/360px-Schloss_Mannheim_1725_v_J_C_Froimon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Schloss_Mannheim_1725_v_J_C_Froimon.jpg/480px-Schloss_Mannheim_1725_v_J_C_Froimon.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Schloss_Mannheim_1725_v_J_C_Froimon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Layout of the <a href="/wiki/Mannheim_Palace" title="Mannheim Palace">Mannheim Palace</a> in 1725</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Karl Philip III. decided subsequently to construct a new palace as his residence on the site of the old "Friedrichsburg". In general, it was part of a trend among the German <a href="/wiki/Prince" title="Prince">princes</a> to construct grand new residences in that <a href="/wiki/Era" title="Era">era</a>.The construction of the palace was commenced solemnly on 2 June 1720. The overall building process was intended to cost about 300,000 <i>Gulden</i>, financed by an extraordinary "palace tax" (<i>Schlossbausteuer</i>), but in the end, the palace cost totalled more than 2,000,000 <a href="/wiki/Guilder" title="Guilder">Gulden</a> and severely worsened the Palatinate's financial situation. The first administrative institutions began using the Mannheim palace in 1725, but Karl Philip III. was able to transfer his court to the new residence only in 1731.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karl_Theodor,_Kurf%C3%BCrst_(1742-1799).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1285" data-file-width="1000" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Karl_Theodor%2C_Kurf%C3%BCrst_%281742-1799%29.jpg/180px-Karl_Theodor%2C_Kurf%C3%BCrst_%281742-1799%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Karl_Theodor%2C_Kurf%C3%BCrst_%281742-1799%29.jpg/270px-Karl_Theodor%2C_Kurf%C3%BCrst_%281742-1799%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Karl_Theodor%2C_Kurf%C3%BCrst_%281742-1799%29.jpg/360px-Karl_Theodor%2C_Kurf%C3%BCrst_%281742-1799%29.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Karl_Theodor,_Kurf%C3%BCrst_(1742-1799).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Charles_Theodore,_Elector_of_Bavaria" title="Charles Theodore, Elector of Bavaria">Charles Theodore</a>, Count Palatine of Sulzbach and Founder of the "Theodoro-Palatinae" as painted by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Georg_Ziesenis" title="Johann Georg Ziesenis">Johann Georg Ziesenis</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The final construction was not completed until 1760. Karl Philip died in 1742 and was succeeded by a distant relative, the young <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Count_Palatine" title="Count Palatine">Count Palatine</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sulzbach_am_Main" title="Sulzbach am Main">Sulzbach</a> and later Duke of <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Theodore,_Elector_of_Bavaria" title="Charles Theodore, Elector of Bavaria">Charles Theodor</a>. Under the initiative of the <a href="/wiki/Alsace" title="Alsace">Alsacian</a> scholar <a href="/wiki/Johann_Daniel_Sch%C3%B6pflin" title="Johann Daniel Schöpflin">Johann Daniel Schöpflin</a> and following the general academization movement in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, Prince Charles Theodor established the Palatine Academy of the Sciences Mannheim (<i>Kurpfälzische Akademie der Wissenschaften</i>) on October 17, 1763. The so-called <i>Academia Electoralis Scientiarum et Elegantiorum Literarum Theodoro-Palatinae</i>, shortened "Theodoro Palatinae" concentrated on the teaching of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_Science" title="Natural Science">Natural Sciences</a> and <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">History</a>, and soon earned itself a reputation which reached far beyond the borders of Charles Theodor's realm. In 1778, a second school was established at Mannheim's palace – the Commercial School (<i>Großherzogliche Handelsschule</i>) – that served as school for merchant sons and which was later named into "Grand-Ducal Commercial Academy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Further associated institutions included the <a href="/wiki/Mannheim_Observatory" title="Mannheim Observatory">Mannheim Observatory</a>, the Ducal Natural History Collection, the Ducal Physical Cabinet, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Botanical_Garden" title="Botanical Garden">Mannheim Palace Botanical Garden</a> and the in 1769 founded Mannheim Academy of Fine Arts. The establishment of the Theodoro-Palatinae had a strong cultural and educational policy link and intended to foster the sciences and arts in the Palatinate. Under Charles Theodor's reign the academy received enormous funding of more than 35,000,000 Gulden and contributed considerably to the cultural, economical and infrastructural development of <a href="/wiki/Southern_Germany" title="Southern Germany">Southern Germany</a> during the second half of the eighteenth century. Charles Theodor developed Mannheim into a German centrum for Arts and Sciences.</p>
<p>Not only the palace, but also the city of Mannheim saw their zenith during Charles Philipp's reign. The glamour of the Elector's court and Mannheim's then famous cultural life lasted until 1778 when Charles Theodor became Elector of Bavaria by inheritance and moved his court to <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a>. Things worsened further during the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, when Mannheim was besieged. During Napoleon's reorganization of Germany, the Electorate of the Palatinate was split up and Mannheim became part of the Grand Duchy of Electorate-Bavaria, thus losing its capital/residence status. Although Mannheim kept the title of "residence", the Mannheim Palace was used merely as accommodation for several administrative bodies. In addition, the reorganization of the Palatinate and Bavaria made the <a href="/wiki/Bavarian_Academy_of_Sciences_and_Humanities" title="Bavarian Academy of Sciences and Humanities">Bavarian Academy of Sciences and Humanities</a> a major concurrent for academic funding. The consequences of religious strife, increased rivalry for funding and the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> put an end to the "Theodoro-Palatinae", which was finally closed on February 17, 1803 after forty years of existence. Several years later in 1817, the "Grand-Ducal Commercial Academy" was closed as well.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Mannheim&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dr._Otto_Beck_-_Senior_mayor_of_Mannheim_(1891-1908).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="571" data-file-width="400" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Dr._Otto_Beck_-_Senior_mayor_of_Mannheim_%281891-1908%29.jpg/140px-Dr._Otto_Beck_-_Senior_mayor_of_Mannheim_%281891-1908%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Dr._Otto_Beck_-_Senior_mayor_of_Mannheim_%281891-1908%29.jpg/210px-Dr._Otto_Beck_-_Senior_mayor_of_Mannheim_%281891-1908%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Dr._Otto_Beck_-_Senior_mayor_of_Mannheim_%281891-1908%29.jpg/280px-Dr._Otto_Beck_-_Senior_mayor_of_Mannheim_%281891-1908%29.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dr._Otto_Beck_-_Senior_mayor_of_Mannheim_(1891-1908).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Otto_Beck" title="Otto Beck">Otto Beck</a> - Senior mayor of Mannheim (1891-1908) and one of the Founding Fathers of the <i>Handelshochschule Mannheim</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>For most of the 19th and early 20th centuries, the palace served no uniform purpose, being used as a representative building and a museum for the city. Although there was no continuous existence of a scientific <a href="/wiki/College" title="College">college</a> in Mannheim, the newly established <i>Handelshochschule Mannheim</i>, or Municipal Commercial College Mannheim, founded in 1907, saw itself in the tradition of Carl Theodor's earlier colleges. The Handelshochschule was founded under an initiative from Mannheim's senior <a href="/wiki/Mayor" title="Mayor">mayor</a> Otto Beck (1846–1908) and the <a href="/wiki/Heidelberg_University" title="Heidelberg University">Heidelberg</a>'s economics professor Eberhard Gothein (1853–1923).</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EberhardGothein1906.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="390" data-file-width="276" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/EberhardGothein1906.jpg/150px-EberhardGothein1906.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/EberhardGothein1906.jpg/225px-EberhardGothein1906.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1d/EberhardGothein1906.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EberhardGothein1906.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Eberhard_Gothein" title="Eberhard Gothein">Eberhard Gothein</a> - National Economist, Professor at the <a href="/wiki/Heidelberg_University" title="Heidelberg University">Heidelberg University</a> and one of the Founders Fathers of the <i>Handelshochschule Mannheim</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although the Handelshochschule quickly developed into a well-known institution that conducted teaching and research in business administration, economics, <a href="/wiki/Pedagogy" title="Pedagogy">pedagogy</a> and psychology it suffered from financial hardship due to scarce financial resources. Since 1932 there had been plans to merge the Handelshochschule Mannheim with the <a href="/wiki/Heidelberg_University" title="Heidelberg University">Heidelberg University</a>, hence both solving the School’s financial situation and supplementing the faculties at Heidelberg with business, psychology and pedagogy departments; nevertheless until 1933 no final decision regarding the integration of the Handelshochschule was made. This situation changed in mid-1933 when the <a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">social democratic</a> municipal administration of Heidelberg and Mannheim was banished and replaced by a national socialistic one that pushed the merging process forward. In contrast to the initial plans featuring a full integration of the Handelshochschule, the <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">national socialistic</a> administration preferred a partial integration only that includes to supplement Heidelberg’s university with psychology and pedagogy institutes from the Handelshochschule Mannheim – in an <a href="/wiki/Aryan" title="Aryan">aryanized</a> outlay however.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Selz.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="374" data-file-width="250" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b3/Selz.gif/140px-Selz.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b3/Selz.gif/210px-Selz.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/b3/Selz.gif 2x" width="140"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Selz.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Otto_Selz" title="Otto Selz">Otto Selz</a> - Last Director of the Handelshochschule Mannheim before its closure</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Director of the Handelshochschule since 1929 was <a href="/wiki/Otto_Selz" title="Otto Selz">Otto Selz</a>, a German <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosopher</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychologist" title="Psychologist">psychologist</a> who is considered as being a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Innovator" title="Innovator">pioneer</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive sciences</a>. As a result of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merger" title="Merger">merger</a> plans and especially due to his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish" title="Jewish">jewish</a> background Selz was discharged on April 6, 1933 following the <i>Badischen Judenerlass</i> administered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NSDAP" title="NSDAP">NSDAP</a> politician <a href="/wiki/Robert_Heinrich_Wagner" title="Robert Heinrich Wagner">Robert Heinrich Wagner</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Waiver" title="Waiver">waiver</a> designed to ban jewish academics from German universities. Later, Selz was deported to the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_concentration_camps" title="Nazi concentration camps">concentration camp</a> <a href="/wiki/Auschwitz_concentration_camp" title="Auschwitz concentration camp">Auschwitz</a> where he was executed in 1943; of the eleven <a href="/wiki/Docent" title="Docent">docents</a> at Mannheim's Handelshochschule that also possessed a jewish background nine shared Selz's fate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The merger process became more concrete in June 1933 when <a href="/wiki/Heidelberg_University_Faculty_of_Philosophy_and_History" title="Heidelberg University Faculty of Philosophy and History">Heidelberg’s Faculty for Philosophy</a> and the University's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychiatric_Hospital" title="Psychiatric Hospital">Psychiatric Hospital</a> discussed about the integration and allocation of Mannheim’s psychology department and concluded that while Heidelberg already has an academically strong psychiatric institution Mannheim’s departments would be a very valuable supplement. After several discussions and internal negotiations, both of Heidelberg University’s departments agreed on establishing a distinct Psychological Institute that fosters the clinical as well as the philosopical perspectives of psychology – hence, the Handelshochschule built the foundation of Heidelberg’s Institute for Psychology within the <a href="/wiki/Heidelberg_University_Faculty_of_Behavioural_Sciences_and_Empirical_Cultural_Sciences" title="Heidelberg University Faculty of Behavioural Sciences and Empirical Cultural Sciences">Heidelberg University Faculty of Behavioural Sciences and Empirical Cultural Sciences</a>. In October 1933 Heidelberg university’s rectorate directed that students of the Handelshochschule Mannheim are allowed to continue their studies in Heidelberg and on October 25 a final meeting between Mannheim and <a href="/wiki/Ministry_(government_department)" title="Ministry (government department)">ministries</a> under Robert Heinrich Wagner was conducted to finalize the merger. The final contract included the transfer of all assets of the Handelshochschule to the Heidelberg University, the transfer of all existing <a href="/wiki/Institute" title="Institute">institutes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Collection_(artwork)" title="Collection (artwork)">collections</a> to Heidelberg as well as the relocation of the Psychological Institute of the from then on former Handelshochschule Mannheim to the <a href="/wiki/Basement" title="Basement">basement</a> rooms of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychiatric_Hospital" title="Psychiatric Hospital">Psychiatric Hospital</a> department at the Heidelberg University. Only two weeks later the whole inventory and staff were transferred from Mannheim to Heidelberg – with this transfer the merging process was completed and the „jews released“ Handelshochschule Mannheim finally closed. While the institutes, collections and personnel had been transferred in 1933 the entire absorption process took until April 1938.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mannheim_Palace_before_1914.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="320" data-file-width="766" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Mannheim_Palace_before_1914.jpg/260px-Mannheim_Palace_before_1914.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Mannheim_Palace_before_1914.jpg/390px-Mannheim_Palace_before_1914.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Mannheim_Palace_before_1914.jpg/520px-Mannheim_Palace_before_1914.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mannheim_Palace_before_1914.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Mannheim Palace before 1914</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although the "Handelshochschule" was closed down in 1933, it built the foundation for what is today known as University of Mannheim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-18">[18]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mannheim" title="Mannheim">Mannheim</a> was heavily bombed from December 1940 until the end of the war and saw more than 150 air raids. The largest raid on Mannheim took place on 5 and 6 September 1943 when a major part of the city was destroyed. In 1944, raids bombed and widely devastated the <a href="/wiki/Mannheim_Palace" title="Mannheim Palace">Mannheim Palace</a>, leaving only one room undamaged out of over 500 – only its external walls survived. Many people supported demolishing it entirely after the war to create additional space for a more modern city architecture. These plans were abolished and the palace was reconstructed instead.</p>
<p>In 1946, the "Handelshochschule" re-opened under its new name <i>Staatliche Wirtschaftshochschule Mannheim</i> (State College for Economics) with a student body of 545 students in the school's first year. University of Mannheim's official seal has its origins during the time of the re-opening of the Handelshochschule. The official seal of the Trustees of the Staatliche "Wirtschaftshochschule" served as the signature and symbol of authenticity on documents issued by the corporation. A request for one was first recorded in a meeting of the trustees in 1946 during which some of the Trustees desired to get a Common Seal for the Use of the Corporation. The seal's design was chosen to represent the strong connection between Mannheim and the University of Mannheim and depicted the Mannheim Palace on top and the square-based outlay of Mannheim's downtown below; surrounded by <i>In Omnibus Veritas</i>, the University's official motto in a shortened version. UMA's motto was based on a line in the <a href="/wiki/Constitution" title="Constitution">constitution</a> from Carl Theodor's Palatine Academy of the Sciences Mannheim from 1763, <i>In Omnibus Veritas Suprema Lex Esto</i> that could be translated into "Truth in everything should be the supreme law". Ten years after its reopening, the "Staatliche Wirtschafshochschule" finally moved into the east wing of the meanwhile rebuilt palace. The other rooms of the old Residence were occupied by government officials whose offices were still in ruins after the <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">war</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1963, the "Staatliche Wirtschafshochschule Mannheim" extended its subject program faculties to a total of three – Business Administration and Social Sciences, Philosophy-Philological Sciences and Law – and subsequently gained the status as "university" on July, 4 in 1967.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Universitaet_Mannheim_Schloss_Ehrenhof.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2609" data-file-width="6069" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Universitaet_Mannheim_Schloss_Ehrenhof.jpg/260px-Universitaet_Mannheim_Schloss_Ehrenhof.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Universitaet_Mannheim_Schloss_Ehrenhof.jpg/390px-Universitaet_Mannheim_Schloss_Ehrenhof.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Universitaet_Mannheim_Schloss_Ehrenhof.jpg/520px-Universitaet_Mannheim_Schloss_Ehrenhof.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Universitaet_Mannheim_Schloss_Ehrenhof.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The University of Mannheim as it appears today</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following the new status the council of ministers of the federal state of <a href="/wiki/Baden-W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Baden-Württemberg">Baden-Württemberg</a> decided to rename the State College into the "University of Mannheim" (<i>Universität Mannheim</i>). The University of Mannheim experienced continuous growth in both recognition and size. While there were only 3,150 students registered in 1967, the number of students tripled by the time of the mid-nineties counting more than 10,000 students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In the winter term of 2013, the university's student body has reached its all-time high with more than 12,000 students. During the growth phase of the University in the 1960s and 1970s not only the number of students but also the number of faculties increased. In 1969, the University of Mannheim expanded its faculty number to eight by adding the faculties of Economics, Geography and Political Sciences and by splitting the faculties of Business Administration and Social Sciences as well as Philosophy-Philological Sciences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Mannheim&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The emphasis at the University of Mannheim has always remained on business and economics, although teaching was broadened to further disciplines. In 2000, the university received as first German and third university in Europe the accreditation by <a href="/wiki/Association_to_Advance_Collegiate_Schools_of_Business" title="Association to Advance Collegiate Schools of Business">Association to Advance Collegiate Schools of Business</a> for its business administration faculty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> In the same year Mannheim initiated the <i>Renaissance des Barockschlosses</i> (Renaissance of the Mannheim Palace), a funding campaign aimed at raising funds for renovating and extending the main-campus. This aim was reached in the financial year 2006–2007, raising €53 million. In recent years, a policy referred to as "profile sharpening" (<i>Profilschärfung</i>) has been introduced to lift the University's reputation in its research clusters to a European level. The closing of majors such as <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a> (2002) and philosophy (2004) in the study program, due to this development <i>back to the roots</i>, has led to frequent complaints from the student council.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> As one of the final steps in this transformation, the University of Mannheim has founded the <a href="/wiki/Mannheim_Business_School" title="Mannheim Business School">Mannheim Business School (MBS)</a> in 2005 to offer executive education in Germany. As a result of the transformation process the organizational structure was restructured and the number of faculties was decreased to six. Since 2007, the University of Mannheim is funded as the smallest university by the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Excellence_Universities" title="German Excellence Universities">Excellence Initiative</a>" under an initiative started by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Ministry_of_Education_and_Research_(Germany)" title="Federal Ministry of Education and Research (Germany)">Federal Ministry of Education and Research</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Forschungsgemeinschaft" title="Deutsche Forschungsgemeinschaft">German Research Foundation</a> to promote cutting-edge research in Germany and subsequently established Germany's first graduate school, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mannheim_Graduate_School_for_Economics_and_Social_Sciences" title="Mannheim Graduate School for Economics and Social Sciences">Mannheim Graduate School for Economics and Social Sciences (GESS)</a> in the same year. In 2008, the University passed a reformation statute to increase its cross-faculty collaboration and research and announced its profile as university with a strong focus on Economics, Business Administration, Social Sciences, Law, Mathematics, Computer Science and Humanities.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campus">Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Mannheim&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Campus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mannheim" title="Mannheim">Mannheim</a></div>
<table cellspacing="5" class="toccolours" style="float: right; margin-left: 1em; margin-right: 2em; font-size: 85%; background:#ADD8E0; color:black; width:30em; max-width: 25%;">
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;">
<p>"The nice and befriending Mannheim that is built evenly and genially."</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;">— <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The University of Mannheim is located on an urban campus in the city of <a href="/wiki/Mannheim" title="Mannheim">Mannheim</a>. Mannheim is a city in southwestern part of Germany and with approximately 315,000 inhabitants, the second-largest city in the state Baden-Württemberg, directly after <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart" title="Stuttgart">Stuttgart</a>. Mannheim is situated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhein-Neckar" title="Rhein-Neckar">Rhine Neckar Triangle</a>, a European <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_area" title="Metropolitan area">metropolitan area</a> with approximately 2.4 million people living there, comprising the neighboring cities of Mannheim, <a href="/wiki/Ludwigshafen" title="Ludwigshafen">Ludwigshafen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heidelberg" title="Heidelberg">Heidelberg</a>, and a number of smaller towns in the perimeter. Mannheim is located at the confluence of the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Neckar" title="Neckar">Neckar</a> in the northwestern corner of Baden-Württemberg. The Rhine separates Mannheim from the city of <a href="/wiki/Ludwigshafen" title="Ludwigshafen">Ludwigshafen</a>, just to the west of it in Rhineland-Palatinate, and the border of Baden-Württemberg with Hesse is just to the north. Mannheim is downstream along the Neckar from the city of Heidelberg. Mannheim is unusual among German cities in that its streets and avenues are laid out in a grid pattern, leading to its nickname <i>die Quadratestadt</i> ("city of the squares"). Its pedestrian zone is a shopping and night life magnet for the surrounding area and beyond. Mannheim is about 30 minutes by train away from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frankfurt_am_Main" title="Frankfurt am Main">Frankfurt am Main</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frankfurt_International_Airport" title="Frankfurt International Airport">Frankfurt International Airport</a>, 40 minutes from <a href="/wiki/Karlsruhe" title="Karlsruhe">Karlsruhe</a> and its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baden_Airpark" title="Baden Airpark">Baden Airpark</a> as well as one hour by train from <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart" title="Stuttgart">Stuttgart</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart_Airport" title="Stuttgart Airport">Stuttgart Airport</a>. Mannheim is well connected to <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a>, which can be reached within one hour by plane from <a href="/wiki/Mannheim_City_Airport" title="Mannheim City Airport">Mannheim City Airport</a>. Mannheim's city centers on the eighteenth century Mannheim Palace, the former home of the Prince-elector of the Palatinate, that now houses the University of Mannheim.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mannheim_Palace">Mannheim Palace</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Mannheim&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Mannheim Palace">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The University of Mannheim belongs to the small group of universities in Germany that are said to maintain a classical university <a href="/wiki/Campus" title="Campus">campus</a>, widely comparable to those of universities in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>. All faculties, research institutes and facilities are placed in very short distances to one another and are reachable by feet. The present core campus covers over 74 acres (0.79 km²) in a contiguous area of Mannheim's downtown district; the older heart of the campus and core building comprises the <a href="/wiki/Mannheim_Palace" title="Mannheim Palace">Mannheim Palace</a>. With a total length of more than 400 meters it is the largest baroque palace in Germany. The palace dates from the 18th century with its Construction commenced solemnly on June 2, 1720. Although the construction was not completed before 1760, it was used by first administrative institutions since 1725. In World War II, the palace was heavily bombed and partly destroyed. Many residents supported demolishing it after the war to create space for a more modern city architecture. Nevertheless, these plans were abolished and in recent years, the palace has been renovated extensively thanks to private donations and government funding totaling more than Euro 54 million. There was considerable building activity in the palace due to Mannheim's anniversary in 2007, when the palace has been repainted in a bright ocher/yellow, the Mittelbau been thoroughly rebuilt (including its new roof construction), and the Ehrenhof yard been restructured and paved with granite. In 2012, Mannheim's campus was listed the most beautiful in Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several of the University's buildings, mainly the Palace itself, were constructed in the <a href="/wiki/Baroque_architecture" title="Baroque architecture">Baroque architecture</a> style from 1650 to the end of the 18th century. Much of the campus, predominantly the Mannheim Palace was designed by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Remy_de_la_Fosse" title="Louis Remy de la Fosse">Louis Remy de la Fosse</a> and Johann Kaspar Herwarthel, a prominent architects and the chief court designer of Charles III Philip. Modern steel and glass elements were integrated and now contrast to the almost 300-year-old walls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> The city center of <a href="/wiki/Mannheim" title="Mannheim">Mannheim</a> is aligned symmetrically to the palace. The site of the palace is impressive, although the construction of roads and railway tracks has diminished its dominating appearance. To the southwest, Mannheim's main campus faces the river <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> and the adjacent city <a href="/wiki/Ludwigshafen" title="Ludwigshafen">Ludwigshafen</a>. To the northeast the University's main campus, the palace, presents its 450 m long front to the Mannheim city centre. The Breite Straße runs from the palace to Mannheim's central square, the Paradeplatz.</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; width:99%; max-width:908px;">
<div class="thumbinner" style="">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SchlossMannheim-Pano-130616.jpg" title="Mannheim's main campus – the Palace in a 180 degrees panoramic view"><img alt="" data-file-height="1582" data-file-width="6977" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/SchlossMannheim-Pano-130616.jpg/900px-SchlossMannheim-Pano-130616.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/SchlossMannheim-Pano-130616.jpg/1350px-SchlossMannheim-Pano-130616.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/SchlossMannheim-Pano-130616.jpg/1800px-SchlossMannheim-Pano-130616.jpg 2x" width="900"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SchlossMannheim-Pano-130616.jpg" title="File:SchlossMannheim-Pano-130616.jpg"></a></div>
Mannheim's main campus – the Palace in a 180 degrees panoramic view</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The central part of the palace represents the <i>Mittelbau</i> which incorporated the representative halls of the Prince-Elector. Today, the Mittelbau holds university library halls and the <i>Rittersaal</i> hall (Great Hall). Furthermore, a palace museum was opened in 2007. The Mittelbau is flanked by the <i>Ehrenhof West</i> and <i>Ehrenhof Ost</i> wings, which include the large <i>Ehrenhof</i> central yard in front of the Mittelbau. In those two wings, there are mainly lecture halls and offices of the university's humanities sections. Below the Ehrenhof, there is a massive <a href="/wiki/Bunker" title="Bunker">bunker</a> complex dating from World War II. Initially constructed as defensive military fortification designed to protect up to 1,500 residents or valued materials from <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied</a> bombing attacks, it served as a <a href="/wiki/Hotel" title="Hotel">hotel</a> with a capacity for up to 65 guests after the war and is used as occasional exposition space for modern and urban art today, e.g. during the <a href="/wiki/Long_Night_of_Museums" title="Long Night of Museums">Long Night of Museums</a> in Mannheim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The Northern wing of the Palace includes the impressive <i>Schlosskirche</i> (Palace Church) and houses the Department of Law as well as Mannheim's <i>Amtsgericht</i> (Lower District Court). The Southern/Eastern wing is much larger than the northern one by including the popular <i>Schneckenhof</i> yard, which is a well-known event ground in Mannheim, and by holding the majority of the university's central institutions, as well as the largest lecture halls and the central library "Hasso Plattner Library". Two sculptures are located on the central courtyard and represent the two founders of the Mannheim Palace <a href="/wiki/Charles_III_Philip,_Elector_Palatine" title="Charles III Philip, Elector Palatine">Charles III Philip, Elector Palatine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Theodore,_Elector_of_Bavaria" title="Charles Theodore, Elector of Bavaria">Charles Theodore, Elector of Bavaria</a>.</p>
<p>Over the next two years until 2017, a new study and conference center will be built in the west wing of the courtyard. The new construction will be built in the disused boiler facilities and coal cellar of the Palace. The study and conference center will feature two semicircular lecture halls, an additional flexible-use conference area, ten breakout rooms and a large foyer that faces the palace garden at the back of the west wing with a large glass façade.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="A5_.2B_B6_Campus">A5 + B6 Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Mannheim&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: A5 + B6 Campus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Besides the palace the university maintains several building in direct or close distance to the main campus. All disciplines which are not housed in the palace are within a few minutes' walking distance. They either have new buildings, like the Department of Economics and the Institute of Computer Science, or, like the Faculty of Social Sciences, are housed in renovated buildings. In 2012, the university and the state Baden-Württemberg has decided to provide the university with several new buildings located in the city quadrates of A5 and B6, which will create a second smaller campus in close neighborhood of the Mannheim palace. The additional area added to Mannheim's campus will be more than 5,100 square meters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The building complex will house parts of the Mannheim Graduate School GESS, economic research facilities and additional lecture halls.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Landmarks">Landmarks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Mannheim&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Landmarks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Contemporary campus landmarks include the <a href="/wiki/Jesuit_Church,_Mannheim" title="Jesuit Church, Mannheim">Mannheim Jesuit Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mannheim_Observatory" title="Mannheim Observatory">Mannheim Observatory</a>, the original Antikensammlung within the <a href="/wiki/Mannheim_Palace" title="Mannheim Palace">Mannheim Palace</a>, the Anna Hoelzel Memorial, the <a href="/wiki/Mannheim_Palace_Church" title="Mannheim Palace Church">Mannheim Palace Church</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ZEW" title="ZEW">Centre for European Economic Research</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Palais_Bretzenheim" title="Palais Bretzenheim">Palais Bretzenheim</a>, the Landgericht Mannheim (district court) and the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mannheim_Schneckenhof&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mannheim Schneckenhof (page does not exist)">Mannheim Schneckenhof</a>.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organisation_and_administration">Organisation and administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Mannheim&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Organisation and administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Mannheim&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Governance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The University of Mannheim is cooperatedly administrated by the <a href="/wiki/Rector_(academia)" title="Rector (academia)">Rectorate</a>, which comprises the Rector (or President), three extraofficial Pro-Rectors (Vice Presidents) and the <a href="/wiki/Chancellor" title="Chancellor">Chancellor</a>. The rectorate is recognized as the 'executive body' of the university. Its main task is to implement the strategic aims concluded by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_Council" title="University Council">University Council</a>. Since October 2012 the UMA is headed by rector <a href="/wiki/Ernst-Ludwig_von_Thadden" title="Ernst-Ludwig von Thadden">Ernst-Ludwig von Thadden</a>. The rectorate further consists of the chancellor, Susann-Annette Storm, who is the head of the central administration, the three pro-rectors, Eva Eckkrammer, Thomas Puhl and Thorsten Meiser who are responsible for <a href="/wiki/International_relations" title="International relations">international relations</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teaching" title="Teaching">teaching</a> and <a href="/wiki/Communication" title="Communication">communication</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Research" title="Research">research</a> and structure respectively. In addition, the Communication &amp; Fundraising Department is also part of the rectorate and represents the interface between the University and the public.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Senate" title="Senate">Senate</a> is the "legislative branch" of the university. The rector and the members of the rectorate are <a href="/wiki/Senate" title="Senate">senators</a> <i>ex officio</i>, as are also the deans of the faculties. Another 18 senators are elected for four-year terms, within the following quotas: nine university professors; three academic staff; three delegates of the student body; and three employees of the university <a href="/wiki/Academic_administration" title="Academic administration">administration</a>. The University Council is the advisory board to the aforementioned entities and encompasses, among others, the director of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Max_Planck_Institute" title="Max Planck Institute">Max Planck Institute</a> Martin Hellwig, as well as CEOs of German industries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JHV_Begruessung_Peter_Frankenberg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="991" data-file-width="779" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/JHV_Begruessung_Peter_Frankenberg.jpg/120px-JHV_Begruessung_Peter_Frankenberg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/JHV_Begruessung_Peter_Frankenberg.jpg/180px-JHV_Begruessung_Peter_Frankenberg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/JHV_Begruessung_Peter_Frankenberg.jpg/240px-JHV_Begruessung_Peter_Frankenberg.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JHV_Begruessung_Peter_Frankenberg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Peter_Frankenberg" title="Peter Frankenberg">Peter Frankenberg</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The rector is the administrative head of UMA and in its chief executive officer, responsible for executive management, representation and leadership on academic issues. The rector reports to and is accountable to the Council. The university’s current rector is <a href="/wiki/Ernst-Ludwig_von_Thadden" title="Ernst-Ludwig von Thadden">Ernst-Ludwig von Thadden</a>.</p>

<p>University of Mannheim is organized as a public, government, nonsectarian institution of higher education. Its official corporate name is "Universität Mannheim". Mannheim's academic activity is organized into "schools", or "<a href="/wiki/Faculty_(division)" title="Faculty (division)">faculties</a>" (<i>Fakultäten</i>). Currently, the University of Mannheim has five official faculties and two academic graduate colleges: The Mannheim Business School (MBS) and Graduate School for Economics and Social Sciences (GESS). At the Mannheim Palace campus, the faculty for business administration, which dates back to the 1969, is the largest faculty with about 4,000 students enrolled, 37 professors, 150 additional academic staff and 37 chairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The University comprises five schools: the Business School, the School of Law and Economics, the School of Social Sciences, the School of Humanities and the School of Mathematics and Computer Science. All the schools are closely interconnected in order to foster an academic and scientific exchange between the different subjects.</p>
<p>The <i>Allgemeiner Studierendenausschuss</i> of the University of Mannheim (<a href="/wiki/AStA" title="AStA">AStA</a>) is the student government for University of Mannheim students and all registered students are members. Its elected leadership consists of the Senate elected by both undergraduate, graduate and postgraduate students, and the President and Vice President elected as a ticket by the entire student body. AStA's task is representing student interests vis-à-vis the university administration and the senate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
